    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_271598074" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/271598074" />
    <a title="Rebecca Ann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4811272-rebecca-ann"><img alt="Rebecca Ann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459815842p2/4811272.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/271598074?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca Ann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca Ann" href="/user/show/4811272-rebecca-ann">Rebecca Ann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4811272-rebecca-ann?shelf=teen-realism">teen-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4811272-rebecca-ann?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4811272-rebecca-ann?shelf=teen-contemporary">teen-contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4811272-rebecca-ann?shelf=favorite-teen">favorite-teen</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer271598074" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14209173413008544020">I loved this book...but I can see why you might either love or hate it. The conflict is multifaceted: a person, a landscape, and the main character's mind. All three of these aspects develop quite slowly, so if you need a faster-paced novel or a clear-cut villain it may not appeal to you. The book also has a lot of symbolic aspects and devices, maybe too many (Titus, the bound princess, the letters...etc). A lot of the plot stretches the limits of realism, so if you come into this looking for a </span>
  <span id="freeText14209173413008544020" style="display:none">I loved this book...but I can see why you might either love or hate it. The conflict is multifaceted: a person, a landscape, and the main character's mind. All three of these aspects develop quite slowly, so if you need a faster-paced novel or a clear-cut villain it may not appeal to you. The book also has a lot of symbolic aspects and devices, maybe too many (Titus, the bound princess, the letters...etc). A lot of the plot stretches the limits of realism, so if you come into this looking for a story that could possibly happen, guess again. <br /><br />To me the heavy presence of the landscape gave this story a gothic element. The antarctic was almost alive, and a character in and of itself. I loved learning all the facts about how it is formed and changes almost as much as the historical details about Scott's expedition. It was like reading non-fiction in a candy coated pill. I'm a sucker for horror and creepiness, so Victor was a great villain for me. Of course it is obvious from the beginning that he's up to no good, but I love finding out all his various tricks and crimes along the way.<br /><br />The main character can be a little grating. Her avoidance of all things sex is a little bit ridiculous, but I found her easier to understand as the story lays out how much damage Victor did to her self esteem and sense of self. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ He groomed her to have daddy-issues so she would look to him unquestioningly, but finding him imperfect she also creates Titus as a better role model.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> It's a little bit hard to "watch" Sym not figure out things that seem obvious to the audience, but her psychological damage partially explains it.<br /><br />Finally Titus. I loved Titus, even though he was undeniably sexist, which means Sym must be as well. The way the author uses Titus to create dialog that couldn't exist otherwise and show us the facets of Sym's feelings and thoughts was brilliant. He also helped lighten or darken the mood respectively. His "presence" pulled the punch of Syms isolation a little.<br /><br /><b>Pace</b>measured, unhurried<br /><b>Characterization</b> dramatic, eccentric, quirky, vivid<br /><b>POV</b> first person following Sym<br /><b>Frame</b>contemporary realism, Antarctic<br /><b>Story Line</b> character-centered, layered, literary and historical references, close-ended, plot-twists, thought-provoking<br /><b>Tone</b> Bleak, foreboding, nightmarish<br /><b>Writing Style</b> elaborate, metaphorical, poetic, thoughtful<br /><br /><b>Red Flags</b>mentions of sex/sensuality, mild cussing, murder<br /><b>Read Alikes</b><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50.Hatchet__Brian_s_Saga___1_" title="Hatchet (Brian's Saga, #1) by Gary Paulsen" rel="nofollow noopener">Hatchet</a> and<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6408862.Stolen_A_Letter_to_My_Captor" title="Stolen A Letter to My Captor by Lucy Christopher" rel="nofollow noopener">Stolen: A Letter to My Captor</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14209173413008544020" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1428660080" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1428660080" />
    <a title="Aj Sterkel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5790437-aj-sterkel"><img alt="Aj Sterkel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450665689p2/5790437.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1428660080?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aj Sterkel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aj Sterkel" href="/user/show/5790437-aj-sterkel">Aj Sterkel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5790437-aj-sterkel?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1428660080" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13428542085538852848">This book reminds me of my childhood. When I was around the age of the main character, Sym, I also had an obsession with Antarctica. Just like Sym, I had a shelf full of “ice books.” I read everything I could about Scott’s failed attempt to reach the South Pole in the early 1900s. Also like Sym, I was more comfortable with my imagination than with real people. It’s eerie to find a fictional character who is so similar to fourteen-year-old me.<br/><br/>Sym is obsessed with Antarctica, so she is thrilled wh</span>
  <span id="freeText13428542085538852848" style="display:none">This book reminds me of my childhood. When I was around the age of the main character, Sym, I also had an obsession with Antarctica. Just like Sym, I had a shelf full of “ice books.” I read everything I could about Scott’s failed attempt to reach the South Pole in the early 1900s. Also like Sym, I was more comfortable with my imagination than with real people. It’s eerie to find a fictional character who is so similar to fourteen-year-old me.<br /><br />Sym is obsessed with Antarctica, so she is thrilled when her uncle takes her on a surprise trip to this frozen wasteland. What she doesn’t know is that her uncle also has an obsession. He believes that there is a hole in Antarctica that leads to the center of the Earth. He’ll do anything to find the hole, even if it puts Sym’s life in danger. Once in Antarctica, the only person who Sym can fully trust is her imaginary boyfriend, Titus Oates.<br /><br />This book is intense and beautifully written. It’s a survival story with amazing descriptions of Antarctica. You’re never sure which characters to trust because they all have selfish motives for being so far from home. They’ll even resort to murder to get what they want. By the end of the book, you can’t even trust Sym because the cold and sensory deprivation of Antarctica makes her hallucinate. I couldn’t stop reading this story. I had to find out how Sym would survive.<br /><br />Sym is a slightly frustrating character because she’s very naïve, and the other characters manipulate her easily, but she’s still a realistic fourteen-year-old. She’s hearing-impaired and not comfortable with herself or her body. A lot of teenagers could probably relate to her.<br /><br />My second-favorite character—after Sym—is Titus. The concept of an imaginary boyfriend is awesome. I haven’t read many books about characters who have imaginary friends. Titus has a dry sense of humor and jokes about his own death. He gives Sym the courage to keep going whenever she wants to give up.<br /><br />I like Sym and Titus, but a few of the other characters are over-the-top. They’re a little cartoonish and unrealistic. I especially feel this way about Uncle Victor. I liked him more at the end of the book than at the beginning, but he still doesn’t seem like a real person. He’s a caricature of a mad scientist.<br /><br />My only other criticism is about the pacing. The book gets repetitive in the middle because Sym spends a lot of time wandering aimlessly around Antarctica. I think the book could have been shorter. The repetitiveness makes it lose some of its intensity.<br /><br />The relationship between Sym and Titus is intriguing, and the setting of this book is one of the more interesting settings I’ve come across in young adult fiction. If you like survival stories, you’ll probably enjoy <i>The White Darkness.</i> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13428542085538852848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('qh4olbUAK0dJrEHBJSBMZKuj1vF6qlU9QkW3jpa8/3ubkM8bj9r2ZnI5Fhq7JF28BZWNIc3pMnMx1Wdg7TWpAQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('IJZ04C84oR4uY6YZIQcLwy+QGspnlgEzzK4pkx/L/XYRGJNuFeJ8PxX28cK/AxobgaZBGtDVZn2/Pvl9ZEKrDA==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1631.Geraldine_McCaughrean">About Geraldine McCaughrean</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1631.Geraldine_McCaughrean"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1296256453p3/1631.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1631.Geraldine_McCaughrean">Geraldine McCaughrean
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
246
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14676863698879030665">Geraldine McCaughrean was born in 1951 and brought up in North London. She studied at Christ Church College of Education, Canterbury and worked in a London publishing house for 10 years before becoming a full-time writer in 1988. She has written over 120 books, 50 short plays for schools, and a radio play.<br/><br/>Her adult novels include Fires’ Astonishment (1990) and The Ideal Wife (1997), but she is bes</span>
  <span id="freeText14676863698879030665" style="display:none">Geraldine McCaughrean was born in 1951 and brought up in North London. She studied at Christ Church College of Education, Canterbury and worked in a London publishing house for 10 years before becoming a full-time writer in 1988. She has written over 120 books, 50 short plays for schools, and a radio play.<br /><br />Her adult novels include Fires’ Astonishment (1990) and The Ideal Wife (1997), but she is best-known for her children’s books. She writes for children of all ages, from first readers, picture books, and younger children’s books, to children’s novels, which include A Little Lower than the Angels (1987), Gold Dust (1993) and Not the End of the World (2004), each of which have won the Whitbread Children’s Book Award, making her the only writer to have won this award three times.<br /><br />Geraldine McCaughrean has also written several collections of stories, including bible stories and fairy tales. She specialises in the retelling of classic tales such as The Canterbury Tales (1984), The Odyssey (1993), Moby Dick (1996) and El Cid (1989) and of myths and legends from around the world. These books include The Orchard Book of Greek Myths (1992) and The Orchard Book of Roman Myths (1999).<br /><br />Geraldine McCaughrean lives in Berkshire. Her book, Not the End of the World, is currently being adapted for the stage. White Darkness (2005), was shortlisted for the 2005 Whitbread Children's Book Award. In 2005, she was chosen to write the official sequel to J. M. Barrie's Peter Pan. Peter Pan in Scarlet was published in 2006. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14676863698879030665" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2918941-the-white-darkness">Quotes from The White Darkness</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;It's true: Everyone needs a reason to stay alive -- someone who justifies your existence. Someone who loves you. Not beyond all reason. Just loves you. Even just shows an interest. Even someone who doesn't exist, or isn't yours. No, no! They don't even have to love you! They just have to be there to love! Target for your arrows. Magnetic Pole to drag on your compass needle and stop it spinning and tell you where you're heading and...Someone to soak up all the yearning. That's what I think.&rdquo;</span>
